  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b4c3c97f-d08e-446a-b927-3dcc6bd13aaa'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer11337061804220163708">1950s Los Angeles: The City of Angels has become the city of the Angel of Death. Communist witch-hunts and insanely violent killings are terrorising the community. Three men are plunged into a maelstrom of violence and deceit when their lives become inextricably linked as each one confronts his own personal darkness. Told with Ellroy's characteristically forceful and relen</span>
  <span id="freeText11337061804220163708" style="display:none">1950s Los Angeles: The City of Angels has become the city of the Angel of Death. Communist witch-hunts and insanely violent killings are terrorising the community. Three men are plunged into a maelstrom of violence and deceit when their lives become inextricably linked as each one confronts his own personal darkness. Told with Ellroy's characteristically forceful and relentless style, <i>The Big Nowhere</i> is the link between the <i>Black Dahlia</i> and <i>LA Confidential</i> in his masterwork, The LA Quartet. It is as powerful and thrilling as crime fiction gets.</span>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_987132').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I would say no. There are a few references to the Dahlia case in the book but there's no overlap with the plot and only one not-very-central character<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I would say no. There are a few references to the Dahlia case in the book but there's no overlap with the plot and only one not-very-central character in both.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_656943388" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/656943388" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/656943388?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780892962839" href="/book/show/1134726.The_Big_Nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=hardboiled">hardboiled</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer656943388" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11294514900259511104"><b>"It was written that I should be loyal to the nightmare of my choice.” Joseph Conrad <i>Heart of Darkness</i>.</b><br/><br/>Newspapers labelled the death of José Gallardo Diaz the <b>Sleepy Lagoon Murder</b> because his unconscious body was found near a local swimming hole. The police arrested 17 Hispanic males for the “murder” even though they had no evidence that a murder had occurred. Diaz was inebriated and eventually died from a fracture at the base of the skull. No one was able to determine exactly how the fracture o</span>
  <span id="freeText11294514900259511104" style="display:none"><b>"It was written that I should be loyal to the nightmare of my choice.” Joseph Conrad <i>Heart of Darkness</i>.</b><br><br>Newspapers labelled the death of José Gallardo Diaz the <b>Sleepy Lagoon Murder</b> because his unconscious body was found near a local swimming hole. The police arrested 17 Hispanic males for the “murder” even though they had no evidence that a murder had occurred. Diaz was inebriated and eventually died from a fracture at the base of the skull. No one was able to determine exactly how the fracture occurred. He could have just fallen or been in a car accident, details are sketchy. Regardless all 17 of the Hispanics were sentenced to varying lengths of jail time. 9 were convicted of second-degree murder and sent to San Quentin. This was 1942. <br><br>It pissed a lot of people off.<br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/zoot-suit_zpsd26acd7b.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556226420i/27413881.jpg" alt=" photo zoot-suit_zpsd26acd7b.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>The “unpatriotic” Zoot-Suits being admired by a police officer and a passing young woman.</b><br><br>In 1943 war has broken out and thousands of White American sailors and marines are stationed in Los Angeles. They find the Zoot Suits worn by the Hispanic males as unpatriotic and too extravagant to be worn during war. Riots broke out with sailors and marines cruising the streets looking for Zoot Suits to slash with razor blades embedded in 2x4s.<br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Zoot-SuitSticks_zps7b599fa5.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556226421i/27413882.jpg" alt=" photo Zoot-SuitSticks_zps7b599fa5.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Sailors and Marines keeping America safe from the invasion of Zoot-Suits.</b><br><br>Sigh. Really?<br><br>Flash forward to 1950s Los Angeles and a mutilated body is found with Zoot Suit Stick wounds. There are realistic fears that if this were to come to light that it could spark another riot. The case is further compounded by the fact that it is suspected the victim is a homosexual. The investigating officer Danny Upshaw already knows how his fellow officers will label the crime.<br><br><i>”PANSY SLASH. QUEER BASH. FRUIT SNUFF. HOMO PASSION JOB.”</i><br><br>Homophobia is a pathological condition in 1950. <br><br>Even though Upshaw is told to let this investigation slide back into the primordial desires from which it sprung... he cannot. He is dealing with conflicting feelings about his own sexual orientation and there are circumstances regarding the death that are beyond just torture, beyond just murder. He is a rising star in the department and when Lieutenant Mal Considine is asked to head up a task force investigating Communism in Hollywood Upshaw is asked to join the team. If he keeps his nose clean he could be the youngest man to ever receive Lieutenants bars. <br><br>You know how it is. Stick with us kid and you’ll go places. <br><br>Now Considine is a piece of work. While he was making the world safe for democracy in Europe his wife was <b>hiding the salami</b> with one of his co-workers Buzz Meeks. He finds out about it and falls in lust with a Czechoslovakian woman who had been the mistress of a Nazi officer that just happens to be on trial. She tells him stories about the sexually perverted things this Nazi used to do to her. <br><br>It turns him on.<br>It infuriates him.<br><br>He shoots the Nazi in the face several times. <br><br>Because he is a rising star, and after all it is only a Nazi the whole incident is swept under the carpet. He brings the woman back to the states and marries her.<br><br>In 1950 she wants a divorce. She tells him that all those things that she told him about the Nazi were made up because she could tell how much it turned him on. He beats her to a pulp. We aren’t talking a few love slaps or a punch or two in anger this is teeth flying, bones breaking, may never look the same again kind of beating. <br><br>It will be alright Mal, your future is still bright. This will all be worked out after all she is barely a citizen.<br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/HowardHughes_zpsf3e5eb65.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556226420i/27413879._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo HowardHughes_zpsf3e5eb65.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Buzz find me something young and fresh.</b><br><br>Buzz Meeks is a disgraced cop for a variety of infractions, but lands on his feet with a job pimping underage girls for Howard Hughes. He also provides some muscle for Mickey Cohen the reigning Jewish Gangster in Los Angeles. Because of his “special talents” mainly doing whatever someone wants to pay him to do he is asked to join the Communist Task Force. He’s not so sure because he was shot up pretty bad a few years ago and he thinks that Considine might have arranged the hit as payback for the before mentioned salami work he did with the ex-wife. Meeks has another problem he is making the beast with two backs with Cohen’s best girl. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/MickeyCohen_zpsbe346f9e.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556226420i/27413880._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo MickeyCohen_zpsbe346f9e.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Buzz, I love you, but I’m going to have to kill you. It can be slow or it can be fast what you say next is going to decide which.</b><br><br>Death wish much?<br><br>More corpses show up. Cops are shot. Betrayal is the natural order of business creating unlikely allies in a race to discover who will die next and who needs to die next. <i>Jazz musicians, burglary, wolverines, heroin, queer escort services.</i>, a promiscuous communist Emma Goldman want-a-be, crooked cops, a woman sleeping with a revolving door of men for the lord, all perfectly natural circumstances to show up in a James Ellroy novel. This is an ambitious novel and Ellroy as always isn’t afraid to hold up the grotesque for us to see. The book is full of interesting, flawed characters who will continue to create involuntary shudders from me for the next several months. Ellroy does have one white knight, but he is so scrambled mentally that he sinks under the constriction of his own confusion. <br><br>I found the book ponderous which is why despite the Herculean effort by Ellroy I landed on three stars. There was so much going on that I felt overwhelmed and wanted Ellroy to wrap up some of the loose threads sooner. The book is subtitled <i>The Red Scare 1950</i> and I wanted more of the investigation into Communism, but the characters became too mired in their personal problems shoving that part of the book to a backburner. <br><br>Ellroy was criticized for being too flagrant with his depiction of a homophobic America. Maybe he does bludgeon the reader with the stupid behavior surrounding homophobia, but then Ellroy always uses a big stick when he is trying to make a point. I enjoyed the other two novels in the L.A. Quartet: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21704.The_Black_Dahlia__L_A__Quartet___1_" title="The Black Dahlia (L.A. Quartet, #1) by James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Black Dahlia</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/57727.L_A__Confidential__L_A__Quartet___3_" title="L.A. Confidential (L.A. Quartet, #3) by James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">L.A. Confidential</a>. The fourth book,<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/101000.White_Jazz__L_A__Quartet___4_" title="White Jazz (L.A. Quartet, #4) by James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">White Jazz</a>, I still need to read and after I shake off some of the weight of this book I will certainly read it as well. <br><br> If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11294514900259511104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_6595506" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6595506" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6595506?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/987132.The_Big_Nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=5-0">5-0</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=whodunit">whodunit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=serial-killers">serial-killers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=hooray-for-hollywood">hooray-for-hollywood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2016-reread">2016-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=la-quartet-1-2">la-quartet-1-2</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6595506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3008335142698217434">Can you dig this, hepcat?<br/><br/>It’s January 1, 1950 in Los Angeles. A witch hunt for commies in the movie industry is gearing up under the guise of patriotism, but its real agenda is to make the careers of the ruthless men running it and help the studios keep labor costs down. Corruption scandals have created a lot of bad blood between the city cops and the county sheriff’s department. Rival gangsters Jack Dragna and Mickey Cohen are fighting for control of the town. Everybody is too busy with their o</span>
  <span id="freeText3008335142698217434" style="display:none">Can you dig this, hepcat?<br /><br />It’s January 1, 1950 in Los Angeles. A witch hunt for commies in the movie industry is gearing up under the guise of patriotism, but its real agenda is to make the careers of the ruthless men running it and help the studios keep labor costs down. Corruption scandals have created a lot of bad blood between the city cops and the county sheriff’s department. Rival gangsters Jack Dragna and Mickey Cohen are fighting for control of the town. Everybody is too busy with their own schemes to care about the brutal murder of a nobody jazz musician. Everybody, that is, except for LASD Deputy Danny Upshaw.<br /><br />Danny is a brilliant young detective with a secret he can't even admit to himself. He recognizes the murder as the work of a true madman and is instantly obsessed with finding the killer, but his investigation is hampered by the jurisdictional feuds between his department and the city cops.<br /><br />Meanwhile LAPD Lieutenant Mal Considine is recruited to work on gathering evidence against Communists for a grand jury, and the job is just the thing he needs to boost his promising career and help him with some family issues. The downside is that he has to work with Buzz Meeks that he’s got an old grudge against. Meeks is an ex-cop who is equally comfortable paying a bribe or cracking a skull with his trusty night stick. He works as a fixer for Howard Hughes, and his cozy relationships with the crooks and film industry folks make him the perfect bag man and troubleshooter for the Red hunting enterprise. Eventually the two investigations intersect, and all three men have to deal with the consequences of who they are and what they’ve done.<br /><br />This isn’t my favorite James Ellroy book, but it is a pivotal one in my own reading history because it’s the first one of his I read after finding a paperback copy at a library sale back before the world moved on so I credit it for turning me onto his work. It’s also a turning point for Ellroy because it’s where he created the template he’d follow for most of his later books. We’ve got an unholy trinity of three men capable of committing monstrous crimes in service of dubious causes to further their own ambitions and obsessions. Eventually circumstances will make them seek to atone for their misdeeds, but their attempts at redemption can be as destructive and blood soaked as the things they already regret. That three character structure and basic story arcs are pretty much the backbone of Ellroy’s career since this one.<br /><br />Ellroy has a tendency to go long and let his plots wander in a hundred directions before gathering up the threads at the end. That gives his books a sprawling and epic feel, but it can also be frustrating and confusing as a reader if you’re trying to keep track of who did what and why. While I love the way that Ellroy mixes fact and fiction so that you feel like you’re reading the secret history that never made the newspapers it seems like he’s also trying to mimic the messiness of real events. It gives it some authenticity, but it sometimes feel like it’s clashing with attempts to fashion in into a coherent crime novel. <br /><br />For me it’s always the characters that keep me coming back to Ellroy, and that’s the case here. There’s a mix of courage and cowardice in all of them, and Buzz remains one of my favorites as the guy who knows all the angles but in typical Ellroy fashion can’t resist doing something incredibly stupid. Danny Upshaw is also intriguing because he’s probably the closest Ellroy has come to having one of his leads be pure and uncorrupted, but even Danny isn’t above beating up a witness for information or committing a crime if it advances his cause. <br /><br />While this doesn’t hit the highs of his best work it’s still a bold creation by a writer that shows the first use of all the elements he’d pull together to hit his peak.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3008335142698217434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1459975965" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1459975965" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1459975965?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780446674379" href="/book/show/820503.The_Big_Nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2016-books">2016-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=pants-shittingly-awesome">pants-shittingly-awesome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1459975965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer173416770506345767">In the midst of the Red Scare, a violated corpse with its eyes gouged out is found and young deputy detective Danny Upshaw catches the case. Meanwhile, Mal Considine is put in charge of rooting out communists in the UAES. Attached to his team are Dudley Smith, a veteran cop with a mean streak a mile wide, and Buzz Meeks, the dirtiest cop in town and the man whom his first wife had an affair with while he was fighting Germans in WWII...<br/><br/>Here we are, the second book in James Ellroy's multi-volume t</span>
  <span id="freeText173416770506345767" style="display:none">In the midst of the Red Scare, a violated corpse with its eyes gouged out is found and young deputy detective Danny Upshaw catches the case. Meanwhile, Mal Considine is put in charge of rooting out communists in the UAES. Attached to his team are Dudley Smith, a veteran cop with a mean streak a mile wide, and Buzz Meeks, the dirtiest cop in town and the man whom his first wife had an affair with while he was fighting Germans in WWII...<br /><br />Here we are, the second book in James Ellroy's multi-volume tale of wholesome family togetherness, the LA Quartet. Sarcasm aside, this was one brutal book.<br /><br />It's hard to sum up a book with this kind of scope. In some ways, this book is the rise and fall of Danny Upshaw, the rise and fall of Mal Considine, and the redemption of Buzz Meeks, three very driven men. Upshaw will do anything to forget about his dark secret, burning the candle at both ends on two cases. Mal Considine needs a big win on the communist front to get custody of his son from his soon-to-be-ex-wife. Buzz Meeks tries to do the right thing despite a lifetime of doing the wrong ones.<br /><br />In some ways, this book reads like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21704.The_Black_Dahlia__L_A__Quartet___1_" title="The Black Dahlia (L.A. Quartet, #1) by James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Black Dahlia</a> 2.0. Ellroy has a few more balls in the air and more damaged men to put through the meat grinder. I knew the communist plot would dovetail with the death of Marty Goines and the others but I had no idea how.<br /><br />As with the previous book, the characters make this a powerful read. Upshaw, Considine, and Meeks were all realistic and believable characters, much more nuanced than most crime fiction leads. Watching them go to their fates was like watching a car flying through a red light at an intersection, holding your breath and hoping nothing catastrophic happens. Meeks, who I dismissed as a disposable dirtbag at the beginning of the tale, wound up being my favorite character.<br /><br />The communist plot didn't do a whole lot for me but the serial killer thread was balls to the wall. As the mystery rocketed toward the finish line, things got pretty tense and I thought about hiding out somewhere to finish it unbeknownst to my coworkers.<br /><br />Ellroy's writing, the bleak offspring of Raymond Chandler and Jim Thompson, makes 1950s Hollywood seem like a shit-smeared labyrinth built on lies and the bodies of the dead. Despair falls like rain and the case played demolition derby with the lives of everyone involved. By the end of the book, I felt like I spent a few days chained to a radiator and beaten with a pipe wrench.<br /><br />While I feel spent after reading it, The Big Nowhere is one hell of great read, both as a thriller and as a work of literature. Five out of five stars.<br /><br /><b>After thought:</b> In a parallel universe, I'm sure this is marketed as the inspiration behind season two of True Detective.</span>
  <a data-text-id="173416770506345767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_109346124" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109346124" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109346124?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/987132.The_Big_Nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12618229-jeff?shelf=thrillers-suspense-spys">thrillers-suspense-spys</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440911027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer550935685753564448">This isn’t your Grandpa’s noir.<br/><br/>James Ellroy is a man so ornery <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6595494' rel='nofollow noopener'>he threatened to strangle Kemper with his lower intestines</a>. Ellroy transforms Noir the hard way:<br/><br/>He snuck up on it in a urine soaked alley outside a sleazy Sunset Strip dive, coldcocked it with a lead pipe, stuffed it in a trunk with rabid chinchillas, drove it to an abandoned warehouse somewhere in beautiful downtown Burbank, strung it up with chains, injected it with heroin, attached electrodes to its ears, jolted it with 1000 volts</span>
  <span id="freeText550935685753564448" style="display:none">This isn’t your Grandpa’s noir.<br /><br />James Ellroy is a man so ornery <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6595494" rel="nofollow noopener">he threatened to strangle Kemper with his lower intestines</a>. Ellroy transforms Noir the hard way:<br /><br />He snuck up on it in a urine soaked alley outside a sleazy Sunset Strip dive, coldcocked it with a lead pipe, stuffed it in a trunk with rabid chinchillas, drove it to an abandoned warehouse somewhere in beautiful downtown Burbank, strung it up with chains, injected it with heroin, attached electrodes to its ears, jolted it with 1000 volts of juice, forced it to watch colorized reruns of Gilligan’s Island*, dismembered it with a cheese grater, fed it to feral kittens, took the remains, sailed out into international waters and used the rest as chum for deep sea fishing.<br /> <br />This is a cold man**.<br /><br />The book is a spare and bleak examination of the dregs of the Los Angeles 5-0. You have the weeding out of Communists in the entertainment world colliding in a perfect storm with grisly homosexual murders. To say this book is charged with bleak visceral energy is an understatement; it was hard to read without taking breaks just to clear the empty pit of despair that started in my belly and threatened to envelop me.<br /><br />The only other book by Ellroy I’ve read was White Jazz which was number 4 in this tetralogy. That book, which was deliberately written in (or edited down to) a clipped style, took care of a loose end <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Dudley Smith got what was coming to him  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> from this book.<br /><br />Recommended for those readers who have no faith in humanity and just want to take their car for a spin in the gutter. The gutter isn’t as smooth as the middle of the road, but in can be far more interesting.<br /><br />*The one that doesn’t include all the names of the castaways in the theme song. Bastards!!<br /><br />** If he has a kitty farm or plays shuffleboard with the elderly, I don't want to know about it.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1000423870" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1000423870" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4677640-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472613597p2/4677640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1000423870?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/4677640-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780892962839" href="/book/show/1134726.The_Big_Nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=urban-gothic">urban-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=2014-reads">2014-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=dicks">dicks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=city-of-angels">city-of-angels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=crime-mystery-thriller">crime-mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=essential-crime">essential-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=author-ellroy">author-ellroy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1000423870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4085268935897948669">Communist witchhunts. B-movie studio westerns. South Central jazz. Hollywood labor union strikes. Mickey Cohen and his feud with Jack Dragna. Queer sex orgies at the Chateau Marmont. Howard Hughes and his penchant for underage girls and crashing airplanes. Friction between the LAPD and the LA County Sheriffs. The Sleepy Lagoon murder and the Zoot Suit Riots. And a sick serial killer that disembowels his homosexual victims by biting into them with animal teeth.<br/><br/>This loaded novel is about all that </span>
  <span id="freeText4085268935897948669" style="display:none">Communist witchhunts. B-movie studio westerns. South Central jazz. Hollywood labor union strikes. Mickey Cohen and his feud with Jack Dragna. Queer sex orgies at the Chateau Marmont. Howard Hughes and his penchant for underage girls and crashing airplanes. Friction between the LAPD and the LA County Sheriffs. The Sleepy Lagoon murder and the Zoot Suit Riots. And a sick serial killer that disembowels his homosexual victims by biting into them with animal teeth.<br /><br />This loaded novel is about all that and more, and skillfully stuffed into a dense and thrilling 400+ pages! This book is really something special and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2887.James_Ellroy" title="James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">James Ellroy</a> is a writer with impressive skill and a great attention to detail. He mixes hard-boiled noir, a complex police procedural, and historical fiction into a stunning story, painting a vivid picture of Los Angeles in 1950, and mixing fictional characters with real life events the way Dennis Lehane does in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2830067.The_Given_Day__Coughlin__1_" title="The Given Day (Coughlin #1) by Dennis Lehane" rel="nofollow noopener">The Given Day</a> and HBO does with many of their shows, like <i>Boardwalk Empire</i>, <i>Rome</i>, and <i>Deadwood</i>.<br /><br />With such complex and detailed plot lines as well as the book's colorful prose, characterization could have totally been left to the wayside. But Ellroy spends just as much time developing the entire cast of complex colorful characters, especially the three leads. The novel follows three law enforcement officers (an LA County Deputy Sheriff, a DA investigator, and a former crooked LAPD officer turned Howard Hughes pimp and bagman) as they get thrown into a grand jury probe against Communist influence in Hollywood as well as an investigation of a series of grisly serial murders. Each man has something to lose, something to hide, and in time, something to fight for. They're complex, you root for them at times and then despise them the next. And Ellroy expertly brings their separate stories together in a web of gripping mystery and tragedy.<br /><br />I love my new home of Los Angeles and I have a fascination with old Tinseltown history and conspiracy, so one would wonder why I took so long to start reading James Ellroy novels. But no worries, with just this book, I'm now on the Ellroy train, and here for the long haul.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4085268935897948669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2277876373" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2277876373" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2277876373?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=mystery-detective-thriller">mystery-detective-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2277876373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5994144874663647846">The focus of James Ellroy’s nasty tetralogy would seem to be the depravity of American life, his particular laser-focus on L. A. The first book was for him personal, connected to the death of his own mother, in his version of The Black Dahlia, set in the late forties. The second book, The Big Nowhere, happens in the early fifties focused on a series of murders of gay men in L. A. and the L. A. wing of the McCarthy trials that devastated Hollywood. Ellroy is, let’s say, a tad cynical about Americ</span>
  <span id="freeText5994144874663647846" style="display:none">The focus of James Ellroy’s nasty tetralogy would seem to be the depravity of American life, his particular laser-focus on L. A. The first book was for him personal, connected to the death of his own mother, in his version of The Black Dahlia, set in the late forties. The second book, The Big Nowhere, happens in the early fifties focused on a series of murders of gay men in L. A. and the L. A. wing of the McCarthy trials that devastated Hollywood. Ellroy is, let’s say, a tad cynical about America. He might just have an opinion about what it might mean to Make America Great Again. (Hint: Great America Never Was). Or if, say, a discussion came up about whether we ought to return to the Cold War fifties, Ozzie and Harriet, sock hops, and so on, Elllroy might just punch you in the face over that one. <br /><br />Make a guess whom Ellroy most admires:<br />a)	cops<br />b)	lawyers<br />c)	criminals<br />d)	Jews<br />e)	Blacks<br />f)	Hispanics<br />g)	commies/pinkos<br />h)	fascists<br />i)	actors<br />j)	politicians<br />k)	women<br />l)	men<br />m)	gay<br />n)	straight<br />o)	killers of any of the above<br />p)	Nobody, in principle (and almost everyone is a criminal, as he sees it) <br /><br />Okay, maybe he likes some women, but he doesn’t necessarily “admire” any of them, though. Maybe we side a bit with the main male detective, Mel, but believe me, he’s no saint, either. He’s in a custody battle for his kid with the wife who dumped him, and she may have had reasons.<br /><br />The title The Big Nowhere would seem to call attention to The Big Sleep, with less romantic pretensions. Like a step down in depravity from that world. This is a noir version of American history, reminding me a bit of Philip Roth’s also pretty dark American Trilogy, also with a historical focus (I Married a Communist goes over roughly the same territory). This is good, though you sort of need to take a shower afterwards. Brutal, graphic action, language, everything. Not a lot of poetry here, let’s say. Along the lines of the tone of the darkest noir, Jim Thompson stuff, but also reminds me a bit of Hunter S. Thompson’s Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas, and plenty of works by Bukowski.<br /><br />This might give you an idea of the loving Ellroy’s venom: <br /><br />“Cliché shouters, sloganeers, fashion-conscious pseudoidealists. Locusts attacking social causes with the wrong information and bogus solutions, their one legit gripe--the Sleepy Lagoon case--almost blown through guilt by association: fellow travelers soliciting actual Party members for picketing and leaflet distribution, nearly discrediting everything the Sleepy Lagoon Defense Committee said and did. Hollywood writers and actors and hangers-on spouting cheap trauma, Pinko platitudes and guilt over raking in big money during the Depression, then penancing the bucks out to spurious leftist causes. People led to Lesnick's couch by their promiscuity and dipshit politics.”<br /><br />I liked the more personal Black Dahlia better, but this is top notch, featuring the following memorable things: The HUAC McCarthy Red Scare “trials,” a series of gay murders, wolverines, Howard Hughes as pedophile, mobster Mickey Cohen, corrupt cops, strippers, prostitutes (are there any female accountants or biologists in Elroy?). Homophobia and Commie-fear-mongering are the central features here, and they are intertwined in the central brutal series of crimes. I’m exhausted from 16 hours of listening that is akin to being knocked around and beaten up. I will now read a series of bunny picture books as an antidote.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5994144874663647846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1350758751" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1350758751" />
    <a title="Aditya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15583553-aditya"><img alt="Aditya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600956683p2/15583553.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1350758751?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aditya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aditya" href="/user/show/15583553-aditya">Aditya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781306744430" href="/book/show/22287230-big-nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15583553-aditya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1350758751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3078884297859449598">Let's get this out of the way <i>The Big Nowhere</i> is not for everyone. It is dark, brutal, ugly, repulsive, despicable and absolutely brilliant. One serial killer victim has a perforated cheek and his severed penis is inserted in it with the rest of it lolling out of his mouth. And it is not even the most brutal murder in the book yet this is in no way a torture porn. I hate authors who only have gratuitous violence going for them - John Connolly, Paolo Bagiculpi. It still might be too much for too </span>
  <span id="freeText3078884297859449598" style="display:none">Let's get this out of the way <i>The Big Nowhere</i> is not for everyone. It is dark, brutal, ugly, repulsive, despicable and absolutely brilliant. One serial killer victim has a perforated cheek and his severed penis is inserted in it with the rest of it lolling out of his mouth. And it is not even the most brutal murder in the book yet this is in no way a torture porn. I hate authors who only have gratuitous violence going for them - John Connolly, Paolo Bagiculpi. It still might be too much for too many readers, hence I am sharing that particular visual before talking about anything else. This occasionally feels filthy specially the solution itself, so keep that in mind before you plunge into Ellroy's contorted creativity.<br /><br />Ellroy debuts his signature structure - multiple protagonists supporting an intricate yet flawless plot. There is some staccato where sentences end in fragments but it is not yet challenging to follow. The stylistic choice is necessary, it is a 500 page book that feels longer in a good way, a lot is happening. It actually creates a crime novel that feels epic and sprawling in scope in a way almost nothing else in the genre does. It comes with an acceptable price of a cast of character so large that some backtracking is needed to keep track of it all (it really helps if you are reading an ebook with a search feature). It recreates 1950s LA in all its rotten splendor, the world bubbling with an authenticity and characters talking in period appropriate cop lingo that it can qualify as quality historical fiction.<br /><br />The plots revolves around a gay serial killer and a politically motivated investigation into communist element in Hollywood. But there is a lot more happening - police politics specially clashes between the Sheriff and Police department, introduction of the best villain in crime fiction - the cult of personality Dudley Smith, gang rivalry between real life mob bosses Mickey Cohen and Jack Dragna. The characters are as good as the plot - complex, interesting and Ellroy is particularly excellent with character motivation.<br /><br />Danny Upshaw is a maverick up and comer cop whose abrasive confidence hides his insecurities. He is obsessed with solving the brutal killings of gay men that the city wants to cover up, his price for continuing the investigation is going undercover to seduce a communist sympathizer except no one has any idea he is a closeted homosexual. Mal Considine, a politically ambitious cop is the closest thing the book has to a nice guy. He is a physical coward, better at administration than he is at investigation and has zero qualms about leading a witch-hunt against effete intellectuals and branding them as subversives. Tuner Meeks over the hill cop and mob fixer is the most traditional noir protagonist. He falls for the wrong woman and then develops a conscience.<br /><br />The characters have distinct voices and obsessions. In fact in some respects, Ellroy's work feels like noir's version of Game of Thrones. More than the actual whodunnit, the real joy is in morally compromised but whip smart characters desperately playing off one another trying to get any last advantage they can. Though if you ever wonder how good the plot is check the initial introduction of the killer after his identity is revealed. See how obvious his initial scene plays in retrospect and how expertly Ellroy misguided his readers. This is a book that really got better with a reread, I felt having some knowledge about the impact of McCarthy's red scare greatly increased my enjoyment the second time around.<br /><br />Some readers will struggle with the overall bleakness, others with an absence of an audience surrogate or a likeable character. I just call it crime fiction for hardcore crime fiction lovers. A crime epic in every sense of the term. Rating - 5/5<br /><br />Quotes: <i>screwed like a rabbit, but didn’t come off as a slut</i> Reducing the femme fatale trope to its bare minimum<br /><br /><i>he'd gut-shot the man with his queerness</i> One of the protagonists boast after blackmailing a gay man. As I said ugly, brutal but real and interesting</span>
  <a data-text-id="3078884297859449598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer733990149" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6816574714108517261"><i>“It all came down to money - the great equalizer and common denominator.”</i> <br/>― James Ellroy, The Big Nowhere<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448087640i/17094197._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='267' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Probably 4.5 stars. I'm leaving room, saving stars, minding the gap, because I KNOW this isn't Elroy's best. Still, it is a novel that if written by any other living crime writer it might be considered their masterpiece and this is only 2nd shelf Ellroy. Chew on that. This is the 2nd book in Ellroy's LA Quartet Series (Starts with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21704.The_Black_Dahlia__L_A__Quartet___1_' title='The Black Dahlia (L.A. Quartet, #1) by James Ellroy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Black Dahlia</a> and includes this, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/57727.L_A__Confidential__L_A__Quartet___3_' title='L.A. Confidential (L.A. Quartet, #3) by James Ellroy' rel='nofollow noopener'>L.A. Confidential</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/101000.White_Jazz__L_A__Quartet___4_' title='White Jazz (L.A. Quartet, #4) by James Ellroy' rel='nofollow noopener'>Whi</a></span>
  <span id="freeText6816574714108517261" style="display:none"><i>“It all came down to money - the great equalizer and common denominator.”</i> <br>― James Ellroy, The Big Nowhere<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448087640i/17094197._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="267" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Probably 4.5 stars. I'm leaving room, saving stars, minding the gap, because I KNOW this isn't Elroy's best. Still, it is a novel that if written by any other living crime writer it might be considered their masterpiece and this is only 2nd shelf Ellroy. Chew on that. This is the 2nd book in Ellroy's LA Quartet Series (Starts with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21704.The_Black_Dahlia__L_A__Quartet___1_" title="The Black Dahlia (L.A. Quartet, #1) by James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Black Dahlia</a> and includes this, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/57727.L_A__Confidential__L_A__Quartet___3_" title="L.A. It really has all you want out of LA Noir. Hollywood, homosexuals, drugs, jazz, sex, the Mob, crooked cops, etc. The plot is near perfect and the characters are BETTER than the plot, in my book, <br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2887.James_Ellroy" title="James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">James Ellroy</a> does for crime thrillers what <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1411964.John_le_Carr_" title="John le Carré" rel="nofollow noopener">John le Carré</a> does for spy thrillers. They both have made their own dark corner of genre fiction literary. Both will be read in 400 years as future academics and fans try to tease out how exactly how fucked-up the 20th century really was.<br></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_793413257" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/793413257" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/793413257?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099537878" href="/book/show/11629308-the-big-nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer793413257" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9690003736982049382">Ellroy has come up with a boldly ambitious and enthrallingly dark brooding crime novel that ticked all the boxes for me. The novel's first half interweaves two stories of lonely, driven lawmen investigating the crimes of social outcasts. In the county sheriff's office, Deputy Danny Upshaw finds that his probe of a series of homosexual murders is unleashing some frightening personal demons. Meanwhile, DA's investigator Mal Considine is assigned to infiltrate a cadre of Hollywood leftists, knowing</span>
  <span id="freeText9690003736982049382" style="display:none">Ellroy has come up with a boldly ambitious and enthrallingly dark brooding crime novel that ticked all the boxes for me. The novel's first half interweaves two stories of lonely, driven lawmen investigating the crimes of social outcasts. In the county sheriff's office, Deputy Danny Upshaw finds that his probe of a series of homosexual murders is unleashing some frightening personal demons. Meanwhile, DA's investigator Mal Considine is assigned to infiltrate a cadre of Hollywood leftists, knowing that in the red-scare atmosphere, any hint of Communist conspiracy he uncovers will advance his career. Impressed by Upshaw's intensity, Considine decides to use him as a decoy to seduce a powerful woman, and the two cases collide, their implications of corruption, deceit and past violence converge explosively. At once taut and densely detailed, this is a mystery with the grim, inexorable pull of a film noir, shot through with a strictly modern dose of brutality, and a very sure sense of place and time. <br /><br />Of all the 'L.A Quartet' this was my favourite, and actually happened to be the first Ellroy novel I experienced. It had everything I was looking for, a great set of characters, a complex plot that had many side stories, the mid-20th century L.A setting was used so authentically, with a wholly gripping and murky atmosphere. The last third I raced through to get to a climax that had me on tenderhooks!. Ellroy is such a great crime writer for the authenticity of this era, and there aren't many that can top him. Wish I could read again for the first time, as second time around just ain't gonna be as good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9690003736982049382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_860390265" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/860390265" />
    <a title="Brandon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3214163-brandon"><img alt="Brandon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603283104p2/3214163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/860390265?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandon" href="/user/show/3214163-brandon">Brandon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=detective-books">detective-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer860390265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15168636792546000311">A body is found near a local swimming hole and the brutality of the murder is frightening. Deputy Danny Upshaw is charged with finding the perp and closing the case. When it’s discovered the victim was gay, Ellroy brings the reader into the homophobic culture of 1950s Los Angeles while pushing Upshaw to his limit in his drive to tag the guilty party.<br/><br/>Elsewhere, both Mal Considine and Buzz Meeks become entwined in the communist red scare. Mal is using it to his advantage in an attempt to advance h</span>
  <span id="freeText15168636792546000311" style="display:none">A body is found near a local swimming hole and the brutality of the murder is frightening. Deputy Danny Upshaw is charged with finding the perp and closing the case. When it’s discovered the victim was gay, Ellroy brings the reader into the homophobic culture of 1950s Los Angeles while pushing Upshaw to his limit in his drive to tag the guilty party.<br /><br />Elsewhere, both Mal Considine and Buzz Meeks become entwined in the communist red scare. Mal is using it to his advantage in an attempt to advance his fledgling career while Buzz Meeks is shaking down unions accused of spreading red propaganda.<br /><br />It isn’t long until all three men are frying together in the same pan.<br /><br />With The Big Nowhere, Ellroy was cooking with all the same ingredients used in The Black Dahlia: the seedy crime culture of 1950s L.A., snappy hard boiled dialogue, and compelling characters. So what was missing? It took me a while to pin it down but I think it eventually boils down to the narrative style. For whatever reason, I seem to prefer my crime fiction told in a first person narrative style. I’m not sure if that’s good or bad but I like to really get inside the head of the protagonist and uncover the case with him/her. I’m not saying that if a crime fiction author chooses the third person style he/she fails immediately; I just find it difficult to become fully immersed in the presented case.<br /><br />Why does it matter so much with this novel? I think it suffers due to my own comparisons with Dahlia and just how much I enjoyed that experience. That being said, The Big Nowhere is itself a compelling story that has everything you could want in a dark, gritty gangland tale. While in my opinion it falls short of the tremendous Black Dahlia, it shares a similar tone and fans of the first novel in the L.A Quartet series should find some enjoyment in its followup.<br /><br />Also posted @ <a href="http://everyreadthing.com/2014/03/04/the-big-nowhere/" rel="nofollow noopener">Every Read Thing</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15168636792546000311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3478377860" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3478377860" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3478377860?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780446674379" href="/book/show/820503.The_Big_Nowhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=historical-crime-fiction">historical-crime-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=crime-fiction-america">crime-fiction-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3478377860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer217703002497806084">Thanks to Ellroy, I was awake long after I'd finished this novel because it was so disturbing on many levels. With two books left to read in the LA Quartet, after finishing <i>The Black Dahlia</i> and <i>The Big Nowhere</i> I had to take a break. They're excellent novels, but even I, someone who lives on a steady reading diet of bleak, had to take a break before going on. I didn't stay away for too long though -- these novels are like serious noir-reading crack.<br/><br/>Full post here: <br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.crimesegments.com/2020/09/the-big-nowhere-by-james-ellroy.html'>http://www.crimesegments.com/202</a></span>
  <span id="freeText217703002497806084" style="display:none">Thanks to Ellroy, I was awake long after I'd finished this novel because it was so disturbing on many levels. With two books left to read in the LA Quartet, after finishing <i>The Black Dahlia</i> and <i>The Big Nowhere</i> I had to take a break. They're excellent novels, but even I, someone who lives on a steady reading diet of bleak, had to take a break before going on. I didn't stay away for too long though -- these novels are like serious noir-reading crack.<br /><br />Full post here: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.crimesegments.com/2020/09/the-big-nowhere-by-james-ellroy.html">http://www.crimesegments.com/2020/09/...</a><br /><br />Once again Ellroy brings us into an LA that is based on a foundation of fact, allowing him to construct his fiction around reality. It's genius, really, when you think about it. But in trying to describe this book or the others, the truth is as Tom Nolan says in his introduction to the Everyman's Library edition of <i>The LA Quartet</i>, "Thumbnail sketches do not suffice" (xii). With <i>The Big Nowhere</i> there is no possible way to encapsulate Ellroy's characterizations, for example, or the movement toward the intersection of the lives of the three main players who all have their own their personal demons to confront while all the while having to contend with forces from the outside. Without giving anything away, it's so hard not to feel some measure of sympathy for each and everyone of the these three people, despite what they've done. It is a book that you feel rather than simply read, and it's visceral. <br /><br /> <i>The Big Nowhere</i> is not perfect, but it is a hell of a ride. Definitely not for the faint of heart. It is not a book I'd choose as an initial leap into noir; it's bleaker than bleak, twisted, unbelievably intense and difficult to read, but I have nothing but serious praise for this novel. <br /><br />I have to add that Dudley Smith is one of the most despicable characters in American literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="217703002497806084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11671618499465596811">Los Angeles, 1950 Red crosscurrents: the Commie Scare and a string of brutal mutilation killings. Gangland intrigue and Hollywood sleaze. Three cops caught in a hellish web of ambition, perversion, and deceit. Danny Upshaw is a Sheriff's deputy stuck with a bunch of snuffs nobody cares about; they're his chance to make his name as a cop... and to sate his darkest curiosities. Mal Considine is D.A.'s Bureau brass. He's climbing on the Red Scare bandwagon to advance his career and to gain custody </span>
  <span id="freeText11671618499465596811" style="display:none">Los Angeles, 1950 Red crosscurrents: the Commie Scare and a string of brutal mutilation killings. Gangland intrigue and Hollywood sleaze. Three cops caught in a hellish web of ambition, perversion, and deceit. Danny Upshaw is a Sheriff's deputy stuck with a bunch of snuffs nobody cares about; they're his chance to make his name as a cop... and to sate his darkest curiosities. Mal Considine is D.A.'s Bureau brass. He's climbing on the Red Scare bandwagon to advance his career and to gain custody of his adopted son, a child he saved from the horror of postwar Europe. Buzz Meeks-bagman, ex-Narco goon, and pimp for Howard Hughes-is fighting communism for the money. All three men have purchased tickets to a nightmare. <br /><br />This was a grand novel of twists and turns, double-crosses and blackmail, and political intrigue all tied together by the master of noir, James Ellroy. It doesn't get any better than this, people! <br /><br />One thing I found interesting was Buzz Meeks' back story. While I haven't read LA Confidential (it's up next), his character was something of a mystery to me in the movie. This cleared up some of the background between him and Capt Dudley Smith. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3627731847374486332">The best Ellroy book, hands down. Better than "Black Dahlia". Picture a fun house-mirrored Hollywood where a psycho killer tears his victims apart wearing dentures made of wolverine fangs, a closet queen vice cop investigating Communist sympathizer movie stars, and a cop who wants to smuggle his kid through Iron Curtain-era Europe during the Redder than Red Communist 1950's. The roller coaster ride of the Big Nowhere lasts for 400+ pages and has no brakes, so hold on tight! </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2193271987">

<div id="review_1735853781" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1735853781" />
    <a title="Emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8575414-emma"><img alt="Emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1441431450p2/8575414.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1735853781?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma" href="/user/show/8575414-emma">Emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8575414-emma?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8575414-emma?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1735853781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6185856641315060859">Wow. This was a powerful book. Dirty politics, policemen, corruption, sleaze, kickbacks and gangsters. Jazz cubs, heroine, pimps and hookers, hitmen. Set amongst the hunt for Red Communists, labour disputes and a series of sex crimes in Los Angeles, Hollywood.<br/>What really made this book for me was the characters- Danny Upshaw, Mal Considine and Buzz Meeks - each with their own demons and motivations- all brilliantly delivered by Ellroy. There is a reaching for redemption too, but whether this is </span>
  <span id="freeText6185856641315060859" style="display:none">Wow. This was a powerful book. Dirty politics, policemen, corruption, sleaze, kickbacks and gangsters. Jazz cubs, heroine, pimps and hookers, hitmen. Set amongst the hunt for Red Communists, labour disputes and a series of sex crimes in Los Angeles, Hollywood.<br />What really made this book for me was the characters- Danny Upshaw, Mal Considine and Buzz Meeks - each with their own demons and motivations- all brilliantly delivered by Ellroy. There is a reaching for redemption too, but whether this is achieved is anyone's guess.<br />This book had all the feels. It made me feel sad, enraged, indignant and shocked. <br />This book was about the big grey area between the black and white. Everyone has a price, everyone is compromised. This is the noir-ist noir I've read so far.<br />And it's easy to read this and think 'well that was corruption THEN, nowadays things are SO different....but maybe they aren't, not really, maybe the players have just changed....paranoia setting in. <br />This is not a happy book. It is not a wonderful book: the contents are too grim for that. But it packs an awfully powerful punch and evokes an era in all its not so glorious colours.<br />Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6185856641315060859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2650569085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13670534154579831091">I have to talk about this book. I just finished it less than five minutes ago and the gears in my mind are still spinning along at full tilt trying to comprehend how goddamn good this book was. How densely plotted it is. How this crime novel managed to make me cry not once, not twice, not three times, BUT FOUR FUCKING TIMES. <br/><br/>Let me make a list of things I want you to know about this book, if you haven’t read it:<br/><br/>1. This book hurts you. Good people lose. Good people die. You will ugly weep.<br/><br/>2. This</span>
  <span id="freeText13670534154579831091" style="display:none">I have to talk about this book. I just finished it less than five minutes ago and the gears in my mind are still spinning along at full tilt trying to comprehend how goddamn good this book was. How densely plotted it is. How this crime novel managed to make me cry not once, not twice, not three times, BUT FOUR FUCKING TIMES. <br /><br />Let me make a list of things I want you to know about this book, if you haven’t read it:<br /><br />1. This book hurts you. Good people lose. Good people die. You will ugly weep.<br /><br />2. This is a great crime novel. One of THE greats.<br /> <br />3. Mal Considine, Danny Upshaw, and Buzz Meeks are three characters I will never forget.<br /><br />4. Dudley Smith is the first fictional character I have ever wanted to bump off. I have to finish this series just to see this no-good, lowdown SOB get his comeuppance.<br /><br />5. Buzz Meeks is my favorite hero I have ever encountered in a book. He is so pure and so real, it is difficult to reconcile that he is a fictional character. <br />He is my hero, and I love him.<br /><br />This book has it all. Crime, a terrifying serial killer on the rampage, corruption, real fuckin’ evil bad guys, and morally complicated yet intrinsically virtuous good guys. <br /><br />Go pick up this book. Thank me later.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13670534154579831091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2802793902" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2802793902" />
    <a title="S.P. Aruna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58833010-s-p-aruna"><img alt="S.P. Aruna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471747692p2/58833010.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2802793902?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S.P. Aruna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S.P. Aruna" href="/user/show/58833010-s-p-aruna">S.P. Aruna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2802793902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5074506702777474473">The plot is about three characters; L.A. Deputy Sheriff Danny Upshaw is trying to capture a brutal sex murderer whilst participating, somewhat reluctantly, in a scheme to expose communists in Hollywood. Turner "Buzz" Meeks, a disgraced former cop, is now working for millionaire Howard Hughes and gangster Mickey Cohen. LAPD lieutenant Malcolm "Mal" Considine, involved in a bitter child custody case, tries with varying success to do the right things in an environment of deception, paranoia and bru</span>
  <span id="freeText5074506702777474473" style="display:none">The plot is about three characters; L.A. Deputy Sheriff Danny Upshaw is trying to capture a brutal sex murderer whilst participating, somewhat reluctantly, in a scheme to expose communists in Hollywood. Turner "Buzz" Meeks, a disgraced former cop, is now working for millionaire Howard Hughes and gangster Mickey Cohen. LAPD lieutenant Malcolm "Mal" Considine, involved in a bitter child custody case, tries with varying success to do the right things in an environment of deception, paranoia and brutality. The story takes place in the aftermath of the notorious Sleepy Lagoon murder case and the resultant Zoot Suit Riots.<br /><br />In this book, we can see the very beginnings of a style which would soon evolve into stream of consciousness staccato. This means that this book is easier to read than some of Ellroy's later novels. The 2 plots, setting up the ammunition for a grand jury hearing against a communist leaning union, and the more exciting hunt for a vicious serial killer, while at first appear to be divergent, converge at the 75% mark. The book was written well enough to keep me going, and may turn out to be one of my favorites of this author.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5074506702777474473" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
